0 
Introduction 
Chapters 1 to 4 introduced each of the main health and care insurance products.  The aim of 
these chapters was to describe how each product worked (ie why you would want to buy each 
product, the benefits provided and how these benefits were paid for). 
Any product means something different to each party involved in buying and selling it.  In 
particular: 
 
Policyholders are interested in exchanging their risk of uncertain cost for the known cost 
of an insurance premium.   
 
The insurance company is interested in acquiring risks that it can pool and manage so as 
to make profits for its shareholders.   
 
The government – often through its regulators – is interested in encouraging and 
maintaining an orderly market for the product.   
 
It is in society’s interest that individuals have the opportunity to control the health and 
care risks in their lives.  If this opportunity is not available, some individuals might fall on 
hard times through no fault of their own, and the government may be faced with 
increased expenditure on welfare benefits.   
All these parties (or ‘stakeholders’) have an interest (or a ‘stake’) in the market for the product, 
hence the term ‘stakeholder interests’.  Since not all stakeholders have the same interests and 
objectives, it is a bit like a game where the different stakeholders compete to achieve their own 
objectives.  The product designs that arise in practice are the results of these games. 
For example, consumers are likely to want to be able to buy a policy that they can understand, 
that meets their needs for risk transfer, and that they can afford.  They probably don’t care 
whether or not the insurance company makes a profit (as long as it is not excessive) or minimises 
the amount of capital needed to support the product.  However, if the insurance company is not 
well run then it may become insolvent, which is something they do care about, but they are not in 
a position to do anything directly about it.  So through the political process, they elect political 
representatives to ensure that the insurance companies are properly regulated. 
In this chapter, we will examine in more detail the stakeholder interests of customers and their 
influence on product design.  Chapter 7 looks at other stakeholders, including insurers and 
regulators.  It also discusses the general factors to consider when designing contracts. 
You may find it helpful to put yourself in the position of each stakeholder and consider things 
from their point of view.  This should be easy for you to do as a potential consumer.  If you have 
worked for an insurance company it should also be easy to do from the point of view of the 
insurer.  The regulator’s point of view may be more difficult to imagine.   
 
 


--- PAGE 209 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 3 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
1 
Customer attraction and clarity 
A product may be attractive to customers if it offers the opportunity to pass on a risk that is a 
worry to them (ie it threatens their plans for an orderly day‐to‐day life), at a price that they think 
is reasonable.   
However, even if a product meets this need, it will be unattractive if it is difficult to understand.  
Lack of clarity may also deter the sale of the product. 
The benefits offered need to be attractive to the market in which the contract will be sold.  
From a customer viewpoint it should:  
 
meet needs 
 
be clear in purpose (in risks covered and in benefits payable) 
 
have a charging structure that the customer finds appropriate (eg the addition of 
options and guarantees) 
 
above all, have an affordable premium level that offers value for money for the 
customer. 
This chapter describes five main factors relating to product design that may affect the level of 
customer attraction and clarity arising from a particular product.  These are: 
 
meeting specific needs 
 
providing cash 
 
providing peace of mind 
 
simplicity and clarity 
 
guaranteed vs reviewable premiums and benefits. 
These five factors are discussed in Sections 2 to 6 both in general and for each of the four main 
products. 
 
 


--- PAGE 210 ---
Page 4 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
2 
To meet specific needs 
2.1 
Introduction 
Health and care policies must be designed with customer needs in mind.  The following example 
illustrates how these needs have changed over time. 
Example 
In the late 19th century in the UK, friendly societies (a form of mutual insurance company) 
provided their members with income protection (IP) insurance.  Benefits were limited to a weekly 
payment during the first (usually) two years of sickness.  The payment ceased on recovery, death, 
or two years from the start of sickness, whichever event occurred first. 
Over one hundred years later, standard IP insurance policies provide no payment during the 
deferred period, eg the first 6 or 12 months of sickness, and then payments are made monthly 
until recovery, death or on reaching normal retirement age, whichever event occurs first. 
Needs of health and care consumers in 1890s 
At that time, there was no social security system providing statutory sickness benefits nor any 
occupational pension schemes providing ill‐heath retirement benefits.  The expectation of life at 
birth was about 45 years.  Both preventative / curative medicine and surgery were very limited 
and there were no antibiotics.  Many people died as the result of illness or infectious diseases. 
There were no national insurance benefits, and so illness was a threat to the livelihood of workers 
and their families from their first day of absence from work.  On becoming ill, the nature of illness 
was such that they could expect to recover quickly or die within a few months.  So workers faced 
the risks of short‐term sickness absence that would mean no weekly wages, or death that would 
leave their family destitute.  Friendly society sickness schemes covered the first of these risks. 
Needs of health and care consumers in 1990s 
Much changed in 100 years.  The expectation of life at birth had increased to more than 80 years, 
so most people expected to survive to retirement in their late 50s or early 60s.  Few people died 
from infectious diseases and there were complete cures for many ailments.  Many other ailments 
were treatable, so that the individual remained alive, but not fully fit to work for many years. 
Statutory welfare schemes and employers provided benefits for at least the first six months of 
sickness.  The employee faced the risk of long‐term sickness absence, perhaps up until retirement 
age.  Many employees were paid monthly rather than weekly.   
IP insurance policies with long deferred periods and monthly payments until the first of death, 
recovery or reaching retirement age covered the sickness risks faced by the employee and there 
was a well‐developed national insurance scheme that provided cover for the (smaller) death risk.   
IP insurance schemes together with statutory sick pay, welfare benefits and ill‐health retirement 
pensions were therefore a good match for needs. 
 


--- PAGE 211 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 5 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
Health and care insurance products are designed with many functions in mind.  In 
particular, they meet three specific needs: 
(1) 
Healthcare insurance products can provide a lump sum benefit that can be used to 
repay loans.  For many, the comfort that insurance will provide protection against 
the inability to meet major financial outgoings of this type is a foremost need. 
Being unable to work and earn enough to pay off a loan is a worry for the customer.  An 
easily understood policy that gets rid of this risk will therefore be attractive. 
(2) 
Healthcare insurance products can replace income. 
(3) 
Healthcare insurance products can finance medical treatment bills. 
In other words, healthcare insurance products either provide cash benefits (in the form of a lump 
sum or a regular income) or indemnity benefits. 
We will now look at each of the four products in more detail. 
2.2 
Income protection insurance 
Main needs 
The main needs that an income protection policy meets are: 
(1) 
Replacement income when policyholders are unable to continue in their own (or 
possibly any) occupation, and cannot earn an income.   
In the short term after the onset of the illness or disability, the employer may retain 
responsibility for the employee’s continuing income.  This may vary from no 
provision at all to six months or possibly longer, depending on the employer’s 
generosity and the nature of local employment law.   
Once this provision runs out, the sick person is dependent on any State welfare 
benefits, on personal financial resources and on insurance.  Insurance is crucial as, 
in most countries, State welfare benefits are unlikely to maintain individuals in their 
prior state of comfort, and personal financial resources will often be limited in 
sustainability.   
By its very name and nature, income protection insurance is fundamentally geared to 
provide a source of regular income for policyholders who can no longer work through 
disability.  In order to provide the claimant with an incentive to return to work, the 
benefits are often limited to a fraction (usually 60% –75%) of gross salary, so this is not 
replacement in full.   
Notice how the interests of the insurer as a stakeholder are recognised in the product 
design.  Restricting benefits to 60% – 75% of pre‐sickness salary provides an incentive for 
the policyholder to return to work and helps in claims management. 
IP insurance may be arranged by the employer on behalf of the workforce or it may 
be down to the individual worker, particularly if self-employed, to purchase the 
insurance. 
 
 


--- PAGE 212 ---
Page 6 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
(2) 
An income stream designed to match the policyholder’s monthly loan servicing 
costs.   
Some IP policies have been designed specifically to meet this need.  Short‐term accident 
and sickness insurance (described in Chapter 1) is an example of this.  Mortgage payment 
protection insurance is a similar product designed to cover the risk of not being able to 
make the repayments on a mortgage. 
The service of loans, such as a mortgage, can prove a major outgo from an 
individual’s disposable income.  This is particularly so when the loan in question is 
the mortgage on one’s own house, for some the largest single financial transaction 
that they ever make.  Because of this fact, many lenders are keen to ensure that 
mortgage payment protection insurance is in place when the loan agreement is 
being negotiated.  This safeguards the loan if the individual cannot work due to 
illness or disability or other defined criteria.   
Earmarked policies like this are often a requirement of the loan arrangement.  The 
individual pays the premiums and the benefits are assigned to the financial institution. 
The benefit will be structured to cover both the interest on the loan and the vehicle 
used to cover the repayment of capital.  A rider to the policy may also cover the risk 
of unemployment.  Care should be taken to ensure the product is appropriate for 
customer needs and circumstances, including health and employment situation. 
An example of a lack of such care in the UK market is where payment protection 
insurance (PPI) was mis‐sold to customers taking out loans from banks.  PPI was intended 
to cover loan repayments in the event of sickness, accident or unemployment.  However, 
it was sometimes sold to inappropriate customers, eg to retired or unemployed 
individuals, and it was not always clearly explained, so customers did not understand 
what they were buying. 
Since the ‘PPI scandal’ was exposed, lenders have had to pay out billions of pounds to 
customers in compensation. 
Again, it may be the individual’s responsibility to organise the cover or be faced with 
meeting the loan outgo from a reduced monthly income.  The benefit level may need 
to be restructured in the face of fluctuating loan interest rates. 
Other needs 
The policyholder may also need a regular income on incapacity to cover premiums that they 
are paying for other types of insurance policy (eg endowment assurance or insured pension 
arrangements).  Because of the small size of the premium and the virtual absence of 
anti-selection, the cost is a small one; it is often an automatic rider on the insurance 
contract itself.  It is rare to take out such cover on a stand-alone basis, except possibly to 
cover substantial pension premium contributions. 
Many small professional practices (doctors, dentists, vets) are dependent on each other’s 
support to provide full services to their clients.  The disability of one partner could seriously 
impact the fee stream from the partnership and provide a risk of client loss.  For these 
reasons, the professionals purchase a form of income protection insurance on each other, 
called Locum Protection insurance.  The benefit provides for the salary and other 
employment costs of a temporary replacement professional, so that the practice can 
continue to provide services to all.  A short deferred period would be applicable.   


--- PAGE 213 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 7 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
In summary, the three groups who perceive the greatest need for income protection 
insurance are: 
(a) 
The employer, who is keen to pass on the financial responsibility for sick employees 
to an insurer after a limited period of invalidity (group IP).  There may be legal 
requirements to do this. 
(b)  
The self-employed person, who does not have the comfort of an employer-
sponsored scheme and who must purchase individual IP cover. 
(c)      Individuals who do not have provision through their employer. 
2.3 
Critical illness insurance 
A variety of needs are met by a critical illness insurance policy: 
(1) 
The benefit can be designed to repay a mortgage or other loan when the 
policyholder is diagnosed with a critical illness. 
Under a critical illness policy, the amount of outstanding loan (whether level ie interest 
only, or reducing ie repayment) can be repaid at the time when the critical illness is 
diagnosed.   
(2) 
Medical costs can be funded when the illness requires surgery or other expensive 
treatment. 
This was the purpose of the original CI policies issued in South Africa. 
(3) 
Income can be provided from the lump sum via an annuity when the individual 
cannot work due to their critical illness. 
(4) 
Business partners can purchase CI policies on the lives of each other such that the 
benefits will fund the buyout of the stake in the partnership when critical illness 
arises. 
 
This is sometimes known as Keyperson cover. 
(5) 
A change of lifestyle can be funded where necessary to improve the claimant’s 
health; for example, moving to a less stressful (and lower-paid) job following a heart 
attack. 
(6) 
Other needs include recuperation or rehabilitation treatment after illness, taxation 
planning, medical aids (for example, the installation of specialist equipment in the 
home to enable the claimant to remain in their house). 
It is important to consider if a critical illness policy is the best means of meeting these 
needs, or whether other insurances may be more focused, or comprehensive, depending on 
what the circumstances demand. 
Question 
In what sense could you argue that PMI is more focused, and IP more comprehensive, than CI? 
 


--- PAGE 214 ---
Page 8 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
Solution 
CI pays a cash sum on the occurrence of one of the serious conditions listed in the policy, 
irrespective of the actual loss suffered by the policyholder. 
PMI is an indemnity product that pays for the actual medical treatment expenses incurred as a 
result of sickness or injury.  The size of the payment is related to the actual loss, and so it is 
arguably more focused.   
IP insurance provides benefits when policyholders are unfit to work in their usual occupation 
(although other definitions can be used).  So, for example, a broken arm may prevent a carpenter 
from working normally and result in an IP payment, but the injury would have been treated in an 
accident and emergency department with no PMI payment and no CI payment (because the injury 
was not serious enough).  So IP insurance is often more comprehensive than CI insurance. 
 
Tiered benefits 
Tiered benefits were described in Chapter 2.  They may exist on CI contracts for which the sum 
insured is linked to the severity of the disease.  Tiered benefits: 
 
may be a closer fit to medical distress and financial needs – less severe conditions are 
likely to cause less medical distress and create smaller financial needs, hence a smaller 
benefit will probably be a better fit. 
 
may be deemed more comprehensive and fairer – benefits will be paid for less severe 
conditions that may not have been eligible for a claim under a standard CI contract. 
A lot will depend on whether the policyholder has to pay more, less or the same amount in 
premiums than for a standard policy covering the same illnesses. 
2.4 
Long‐term care insurance 
The main need being met by LTCI is the provision of some financial protection against the 
costs of care and assistance when individuals become unable to look after themselves.  
Typically this arrives in old age; this at least is where the purchaser of LTCI will be focusing 
attention. 
There may be uncertainty as to the role of the State in the future in paying for care – it will 
provide comfort to the person to know that there is an independent source of cash that will 
be triggered when severe incapacity sets in. 
Furthermore, with changing social and demographic circumstances some individuals might be 
worried about the reduction in informal care as well as in care provided by the State. 
Claimants’ needs at the time may entail domestic support, eg a nurse or other carer visiting 
the patient’s home periodically to monitor wellbeing.  This may progress to live-in care as 
the claimant becomes more incapacitated.  Alternatively, residential care may be sought in 
establishments that can provide various levels of care and vigilance.   


--- PAGE 215 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 9 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
In some countries, continuing care retirement communities (CCRCs) have become popular.  
People join a community usually by paying a lump sum, often raised from the sale of their current 
house.  Within the community they receive accommodation and care appropriate to their needs 
in return for a regular service charge.  More comprehensive care (eg 24‐hour nursing) may entail 
extra charges that could be funded from a LTCI policy. 
Finally, there may be a need for medical care, where physical (and/or mental) breakdown 
requires the intervention and supervision of doctors and nursing staff.  This last stage will 
generally be the most expensive. 
Question 
Would a PMI policy be appropriate insurance to cover the medical care described in the last 
paragraph? 
Solution 
Treatment that involves long‐term care is, by its definition, the treatment of a chronic condition.  
Payments for the treatment of chronic conditions are normally excluded from a PMI policy, so 
most conditions would not be covered.  However, the treatment of acute episodes, while the 
policyholder is receiving long‐term care, would be covered by a PMI policy. 
 
At the early stages of incapacity, some of this care and attention can be provided by family 
or friends.  However, for many, LTCI is bought to avoid dependence on the loyalty of unpaid 
care in this way. 
Even though informal carers do not usually receive wages, there will be extra expenses connected 
with their caring that could be met from a LTCI policy with cash benefits.  In some countries, the 
State may also provide benefits for carers (eg paying an allowance or paying tax relief for 
payments to carers).  However, the benefits under a LTCI policy are often defined in cash terms 
without directly meeting the needs of claimants. 
Here we have a conflict between the individuals’ needs (they would like a policy that provides 
care as and when it is needed) and the insurers’ needs (they are reluctant to provide care because 
the amount needed and its cost in the future are very difficult to forecast).  Some insurers in the 
market may offer care benefits, but for a high premium.  Other insurers may offer cash benefits 
but for a more modest premium.  The reluctance of consumers to pay high premiums then results 
in a predominance of cash provision in the market. 
2.5 
Private medical insurance 
Private medical insurance aims to assist the policyholder in covering the cost of medical 
care.  This will meet needs directly where there is no State alternative available to the 
policyholder – either because a State alternative does not exist, or because personal wealth 
levels are above eligibility criteria.   


--- PAGE 216 ---
Page 10 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
In many territories, the State will provide some level of healthcare to all; PMI is then bought 
when the individual wants a higher level of care, such as: 
 
medical attention without waiting 
 
medical attention in higher standard of accommodation eg private room 
 
medical attention with doctor of choice 
 
medical attention in a local hospital. 
Recall that PMI is a contract that focuses on normal hospital needs, though it can also cover 
private doctor charges or primary care in some territories. 
If no State-funded healthcare is provided, PMI will usually pay for all forms of healthcare 
needs on an indemnity basis.  It will cover primary care (such as visiting the family doctor 
or nurse) and hospital care for all forms of chronic and acute illnesses. 
Question 
Products need to be designed so that they are easily understood.  Describe two common 
misunderstandings among consumers about PMI insurance products. 
Solution 
Consumers believe that PMI policies will pay for all medical care and not just for care related to 
acute conditions. 
Some consumers may believe that PMI is a long‐term policy with guaranteed premiums (like some 
CI insurance policies), and not a one‐year policy with reviewable premiums like motor insurance. 
 
2.6 
Microinsurance 
Microinsurance is insurance that is targeted towards those who are working, but with low 
incomes.   
Those on low incomes are more vulnerable to adverse events, having less in savings to support 
themselves in times of need.  Microinsurance helps to avoid the need for such individuals to rely 
on money lenders, who may be expensive and unscrupulous.  Some provision of health cover can 
be especially reassuring to families, particularly in countries where the State welfare support 
system is limited.   
Therefore it is designed to meet needs that are similar to those noted above, but with 
greater emphasis on the more basic components and on affordability.   
It is characterised by limited benefits and very low premiums, for reasons of affordability.   
The considerations set out in the following sections (providing peace of mind to 
dependants and the need for simplicity) are also highly relevant. 
Microinsurance is not a product type in itself; it is the fact that it is aimed at those on low incomes 
that makes it microinsurance.   


--- PAGE 217 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 11 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The main health‐related microinsurance products are versions of PMI (eg contributing to the costs 
of hospitalisation, primary health insurance or maternity care), and IP (ie providing financial 
protection in the event of inability to work due to an illness or injury). 
Microinsurance is sometimes sold alongside ‘microfinance’ (a source of financial services for 
entrepreneurs and small businesses lacking access to banking and related services).  So an 
individual can borrow a small sum of money to help set up a business, and can insure the 
repayments against death or sickness.  Lenders may only be willing to give such loans if insurance 
is in place. 
By its nature, microinsurance is particularly important for the developing world and is 
currently well developed in India and some parts of Africa.   
There are also health insurance microinsurance schemes operating in Senegal, Bangladesh, 
Vietnam, Zambia and Uganda. 
It is covered in more detail at the Specialist Advanced level.

SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
3 
To provide cash 
3.1 
Introduction 
Cash vs indemnity 
Some health and care insurance products provide indemnity benefits, and so pay for the full cost 
of treatment or care that the policyholder has received.  The future benefit amounts are 
unknown, and payments will often be made directly to the treatment or care provider 
(eg hospital or nursing home). 
However, most types of healthcare insurance products provide a cash benefit, as opposed 
to an indemnity benefit. 
Cash gives the policyholder choice.  It can be directly applied to meet personal needs, 
without being earmarked for particular purposes.  If the benefit is provided in lump sum 
form then it can be converted to produce income, with or without capital protection. 
Policyholders can decide, within reason and subject to income protection replacement 
ratios, what level of cash benefit meets their needs.  The broker or other financial 
intermediary will advise the purchaser on benefit levels to satisfy certain needs and will 
review these amounts periodically as needs and financial circumstances change.  The 
underwriter will watch carefully for over-insurance.   
Lump sum vs income stream 
The cash benefit can be in the form of either a lump sum (eg critical illness insurance) or 
income stream (eg income protection insurance), on meeting the relevant claim conditions.  
However, receiving a cash benefit means that the customer needs to understand the typical 
costs (eg of medical treatment) to decide on the appropriate cover.  Additionally, the 
customer may need to make decisions about what to spend a cash benefit on when they are 
sick and are not so able to make sound decisions.   
By contrast, an advantage of indemnity benefits is that an insurer may be able to obtain 
cheaper prices from healthcare providers due to the volume of business given to providers. 
So it might be easier and cheaper to obtain medical treatment under PMI than from the proceeds 
of a non‐indemnity based policy, such as CI or MME. 
Financing recuperation needs 
Some products go further than the attempt to meet financial outgo when disabled or 
receiving treatment.   
Many companies have strong marketing messages that their products also provide cash to 
finance recuperation needs, before the policyholder is fit to return to full employment. 
When unable to work due to long‐term sickness, many policyholders find the offer of personal 
support, advice and counselling attractive.  Extra benefits like this are often valued highly by 
prospective policyholders yet add little to the insurance company’s costs.  They can therefore be 
important in increasing the attraction of a product and thus increasing sales. 


--- PAGE 219 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 13 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
3.2 
Income protection insurance 
Cash vs indemnity 
IP provides cash benefits. 
Lump sum vs income stream 
Under income protection (IP) insurance the benefit is in the form of regular income 
payments.  This may be commutable to provide a lump sum, but this is not generally the 
case.  If commutation is provided, then the insurer must assume that the claimant is not 
anticipating a long claim duration and will value the commutation accordingly – unless the 
incapacity is expected to be permanent.   
Here, the Core Reading is saying that the insurer will calculate the terms for the conversion 
allowing for the potential anti‐selection risk.  If policyholders think they will be off work for a long 
time then they might prefer to receive the regular payments.  However, if policyholders expect to 
be back at work relatively quickly, then they might think taking a cash lump sum will offer better 
terms and try to select against the insurer. 
Financing recuperation needs 
IP products may provide a rehabilitation or proportional benefit to those who return to work 
on a part-time basis or in a less strenuous (and lower paid) role.  This can benefit both the 
insurer (as return to work is encouraged) and the policyholder (whose path to full recovery 
is hastened). 
3.3 
Critical illness insurance 
Cash vs indemnity 
CI provides cash benefits. 
Lump sum vs income stream 
One of the attractions of critical illness (CI) insurance, over income protection insurance, is 
the receipt of a lump sum, as opposed to an income stream where the benefits can be 
withdrawn by the insurer.  As described in Chapter 2, the trigger for payment is typically the 
diagnosis of a specified illness. 
So CI provides a lump sum, while IP provides an income stream. 
Although the lump sum could be converted to income by the purchase of an annuity or by 
placing the cash on deposit, the adviser must make it clear that critical illness and income 
protection policies are not direct substitutes.   
Note that a CI insurance lump sum is often used to pay off a mortgage, which removes the need 
for an income stream for regular mortgage repayments. 


--- PAGE 220 ---
Page 14 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
Financing recuperation needs 
Critical illness policies will often pay out more than the cost of immediate care; the surplus is then 
available for recuperation or rehabilitation treatment.  For example, the additional amount could 
be used to finance a change of lifestyle to improve the claimant’s health, eg moving to a less 
stressful (and lower‐paid) job following a heart attack. 
It can be the case that the cash benefit under CI represents a genuine windfall to the 
policyholder, in that the amount of money produced can far outweigh the medical costs or 
the long-term damage to quality of life (although this is inherently difficult to quantify, and 
some policyholders may deem any such damage to be worthy of a very high compensation).  
In this way, it is not directly related to needs.  This can cause anti-selection problems and 
conflicts with one of the basic principles of insurance: the matching of loss to benefit. 
This contrasts with an IP policy where benefit stops on recovery, and a partial recovery might 
result in the policyholder working part‐time and receiving a proportionately reduced benefit. 
Example 
Angioplasty is an invasive procedure performed to reduce or eliminate blockages in coronary 
arteries.  Opening a blockage in a coronary artery typically involves the use of an angioplasty 
balloon.  Often a ‘stent’ is implanted after angioplasty to keep the artery open and prevent 
blockage occurring again.  The arteries are usually accessed through a needle puncture (the 
‘keyhole’) made in the groin (femoral artery).  In simple cases, the procedure can take just 30 
minutes. 
Before the development of keyhole surgery the treatment of this condition required major 
surgery and was a critical illness.  However advances in surgical techniques have made the 
treatment a minor procedure. 
As the sum insured is much larger than the costs of the treatment and any losses resulting from 
the condition, windfall gains have resulted. 
There may well be a temptation for the policyholder to undergo treatment of marginal necessity 
in order to benefit from the policy.  In response to this risk, several insurers have now removed 
angioplasty from the list of insured conditions.   
 
Question 
Suggest how the terms and conditions of a CI policy might be amended so that the same range of 
illnesses and procedures are covered, but the problems highlighted in the example above are 
avoided. 
Solution 
The policy could recognise that not all serious injuries and illnesses are equally severe and offer a 
tiered benefit CI product. 
 


--- PAGE 221 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 15 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
3.4 
Long‐term care insurance 
Cash vs indemnity 
LTCI may provide cash or indemnity benefits. 
The main need of the individual in this case may be an indemnity benefit that would cover 
the cost of care in the future, should the individual require it.  However, few insurers (if any) 
will fund for care, ie will promise to indemnify the policyholder’s residential and medical 
costs from incapacity in the future for the balance of lifetime, since this is deemed to be too 
uncertain a liability.  The insurer will generally take a longevity (annuity) risk and may 
additionally promise to increase benefit levels in line with a suitable objective index of costs 
– but this is far short of indemnification.   
The uncertainties mentioned above surround the probability of a claim, the duration of the claim 
and the amount of the claim payment(s). 
The probability of claims will depend on: 
 
the types of accidents and illnesses that result in a need for care in the future 
 
new methods of treatment (eg a cure for Alzheimer’s disease) and advances in care 
technology (eg improved post‐stroke care). 
The duration of claims may also be affected by advances in medical treatment, for example, they 
may prolong the policyholder’s life when they are receiving care.   
The amount of claim payments will depend on: 
 
the costs of care (eg salaries of care workers)  
 
how care is provided (eg more high‐tech care may be more expensive) 
 
changes in the supply and demand for care services. 
It is difficult (if not impossible) to forecast how these factors will change, therefore they may be 
viewed as too uncertain to entertain by many insurers.  
Lump sum vs income stream 
Long-term care insurance generally provides a cash income, triggered by the onset of 
incapacity.  The benefit level is chosen at the time of purchase to meet estimated levels of 
assistance at the time of need, in accordance with the policyholder’s means to pay 
(estimated income). 
This describes a pre‐funded policy. 
Alternatively, an immediate needs policy converts a lump sum into a lifetime income, 
dependent on level of disability, at the time of need.  Here also the level of benefit may 
increase with worsening incapacity. 


--- PAGE 222 ---
Page 16 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
3.5 
Private medical insurance 
Cash vs indemnity 
Private medical insurance is normally an indemnity product – it pays the medical treatment 
costs incurred by the policyholder and in many cases the insurer settles with the provider 
of treatment directly.  As described in Chapter 4, the insured event is treatment rather than 
the diagnosis of an illness or condition. 
It should be noted that some PMI products have excesses or upper limits and hence will not 
provide full indemnity. 
Only under special circumstances will the plan provide cash, such as: 
 
Health cash plans are special arrangements designed to provide cash when certain 
medical events take place (eg physiotherapy, new spectacles). 
Other examples of health‐related events are a stay in hospital and the undergoing of 
dental treatment. 
The benefits under these plans are deliberately designed to provide a contribution to 
policyholders’ medical costs, and not to provide full indemnification of these expenses; in 
this way the risk of anti‐selection is much diminished. 
 
In territories where the State offers an alternative, the insurer may pay a daily benefit 
in cash for time spent by the policyholder in a State hospital, which does not 
otherwise incur cost for the insurer. 
Such benefits are advertised to encourage individuals to reduce the insurer’s claim outgo, 
by taking advantage of State‐provided treatment where available.   
 
Major medical expense plans, which provide a lump sum when the policyholder 
undergoes surgery. 
 
Personal accident plans, which provide a lump sum when the policyholder suffers a 
specified injury. 
There may also be a cap on the fees paid by the insurer for certain procedures, meaning 
that the benefit is not full indemnity.  The policyholder may need to cover the difference if 
the hospital and/or consultant are charging more than the maximum.   
 
 


--- PAGE 223 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 17 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
4 
To provide peace of mind 
4.1 
Introduction 
You can only be concerned about something if you are aware of it.  So customers’ concerns only 
relate to those risks that they recognise.   
Customers are more aware of some risks in their lives than they are of others.  
For example, in the UK, individuals are generally aware that the State provides treatment (under 
the national health service) to people who fall ill, but that it does not provide lump sum payments 
when this happens.  However, many individuals believe that if people are unable to work, then 
the State will provide generous sickness benefits (when in practice it only provides a relatively low 
level).   
The result is that many consumers may not be too concerned that they will be without an income 
if they are unable to work for an extended period because of sickness or injury. 
Furthermore, individuals may also be very bad at estimating the size of the risks they face.  For 
example, many individuals believe that they are more likely to get a serious illness than to 
experience long‐term sickness that prevents them from working.  So in some markets CI insurance 
sells much better than IP. 
Ill-health is unknown territory to many people and can be an area of much concern.  
Individuals, especially those who have others financially dependent on them, will wish to 
minimise the risk that a breakdown in healthcare will disrupt their financial wellbeing.  
Disability and sickness are often unpredictable and treatment bills can be of unknown size.  
Many value the insurers’ willingness to provide financial comfort in these circumstances.  
The psychological effect of knowing that you are protected from the financial consequences 
of ill-health definitely fulfils a policyholder need. 
However, in many territories, the insurer may be unwilling to indemnify all future outgoings 
in all products.  For example, few are willing to guarantee the cost of long-term care in old 
age, preferring to estimate and fund for an appropriate amount of cash. 
Example 
Here is a description of a LTCI product sold in North America. 
The New York State Partnership for Long‐Term Care is a public‐private partnership, which links 
Medicaid and private long‐term care insurance.  Medicaid is the State health and care provider. 
The private part of these policies is the insurance coverage for long‐term care.  The public part is 
Medicaid extended coverage for long‐term care and other Medicaid services, eg primary medical 
care.  Both parts help to protect an individual’s assets. 


--- PAGE 224 ---
Page 18 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
The private part of the long‐term care partnership policies must cover at least three years of 
nursing home care, six years of home care or an equivalent combination of both.  This is the 
minimum benefit duration.  Once the minimum benefit duration is reached, regardless of 
whether the policy’s benefits have been exhausted, policyholders will be eligible for Medicaid 
extended coverage for the remainder of their life without consideration of their assets (which are 
fully protected).  Variants of the product are available, offering shorter benefit durations and less 
asset protection. 
There are three main stakeholders: individuals, the insurer and the State government: 
 
Individual have a need to provide for long‐term care costs in old age, but do not want to 
spend all their assets on care and so be unable to make bequests to family and friends.   
 
The State would like those who can afford it to provide for their own care costs and not 
rely on the State.   
 
Insurers find it difficult to forecast the eventual costs of care and so are reluctant to sell 
policies that cover all the care that is needed, preferring to provide cash‐limited benefits. 
So the State offers to provide for those who need care for an extended period, if individuals buy 
insurance that provides care for an initial period.  The insurers can then more easily forecast the 
maximum cost of the benefits needed and so can provide competitively‐priced insurance.  The 
State will also regulate insurers and approve qualifying policies, so that individuals find it 
straightforward to choose and buy a policy from those available.  As a result, all the stakeholders 
achieve their key aims. 
 
4.2 
Income protection insurance 
The individual with dependants (spouse and/or children) needs the comfort of knowing that 
there will be an income stream into the long term to provide for their upkeep and welfare, if 
the individual is unable to work, or must take a lower-paid job, due to ill health.   
This is the motivation that encourages purchase of income protection insurance.  State 
sickness or disability allowances are often insufficient as replacement income and 
employment benefits are often only available in the short-term.   
Repayment of high cost loans such as mortgages is another serious consideration when 
the normal income supply is removed.  The policyholder needs to examine all potential 
outgo when choosing a benefit level. 
4.3 
Critical illness insurance 
Critical illness insurance is based on the diagnosis of a certain number of headline diseases 
or on the occurrence of a number of serious surgical procedures.  It can thus provide 
financial peace of mind.   
However, it is not comprehensive in the diseases and procedures covered.  A combination 
of IP and PMI might be better targeted and more comprehensive.   


--- PAGE 225 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 19 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
4.4 
Long‐term care insurance 
Individuals may be concerned that they will not have sufficient funds to pay for their care in 
old age and that the care available from the State is either not available or inadequate.  
Possibly they do not want to rely on family and friends to provide help. 
Policies may also be purchased by people who wish to fund for the care of elderly relatives 
and friends.  Their concern is not having enough money or time, when the need arises, to 
look after an elderly person for whose welfare they are (or feel they are) responsible. 
Long-term care insurance goes some way to providing peace of mind in these respects; but 
the policyholder is left with the concern that the funds that the insurer will provide are not 
enough to provide the care at the level deemed appropriate in the future. 
Policies that only provide cash benefits create another uncertainty for the policyholder.  When 
they are in need of care, despite the fact that they have the cash benefits from the policy, they 
and their relatives will have little or no experience of buying care services (eg judging price and 
quality of the services offered, and knowing how State‐provided care interacts with 
privately‐purchased care).  Some insurers have addressed this concern by offering a care 
management service to provide help and advice without charge. 
The need for long-term care insurance is often one of priority.  While in employment, other 
needs such as mortgage repayment and pension funding take a higher priority.  A growing 
awareness for LTCI is often felt after retirement, at which point the funding for long-term 
care becomes more expensive. 
4.5 
Private medical insurance 
Where the State does not offer medical care for an individual, the concern underpinning PMI 
purchase is the basic one that the cost of treatment may exceed disposable funds or may 
be at a level to cause financial discomfort.   
Where the State does provide the alternative, the purchaser may buy PMI due to a fear of 
not being able to work and generate income while waiting for State medical treatment.   
PMI would reduce the time spent waiting for treatment, providing peace of mind to some extent. 
The purchaser may also have concerns about the quality of treatment and/or 
accommodation in the State sector and therefore be prepared to pay for better medical 
treatment. 
Question 
Who else might share the concern of being ‘not being able to work and generate income’? 
Solution 
Your employer might be particularly concerned if you are unable to work, particularly if you fulfil 
a vital role in the business.  This might be especially important for a small business where roles in 
the business are not duplicated (eg one bricklayer in a small building business) or where each 
person generates fee income for the business (eg partners in a veterinary practice). 
 


--- PAGE 226 ---
Page 20 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
5 
Simplicity and clarity 
5.1 
Introduction 
Life insurance is relatively straightforward – a sum insured is payable on death.  Healthcare 
insurances cover events that are more complex and benefits that can vary according to the 
severity of the episode.   
5.2 
Income protection insurance 
The income protection concept is simple and appealing – a replacement income at a time of 
great need.  But there are several issues that the insurer must consider to protect against 
greater than expected losses: 
(1) 
Benefit limits must be applied (usually defined with reference to pre-disability 
salaries) to ensure that the individual has an incentive to return to work.  Policies 
will often have a maximum benefit specified in the contract conditions; this will be 
expressed in terms of salary at the time of claim and State benefits at the time of 
claim.  The actual replacement ratio will vary depending on salary and individual 
circumstances. 
For example, the benefits payable might be restricted to the smaller of the sum insured or 
to 60% of income immediately prior to the period of illness.  Any other benefits – such as 
State benefits – that the policyholder might be receiving while unable to work, would also 
be deducted from the payments made under the policy.   
(2) 
Payouts are not always linked to current salary (especially for individual IP policies 
where benefits are often expressed as a cash amount rather than as a percentage of 
salary) and this should prompt annual review of benefit levels prior to disability 
occurring.  However, this is more an issue for policyholders and their advisers. 
(3) 
Underwriting can be complex.  Certain occupations are difficult to cover because of 
the high potential disability risk.  Some individuals may have difficulty getting 
accepted for insurance because of their own medical experience or because of 
family medical history. 
(4) 
There may be circumstances in which claims will not be paid.  Exclusions may 
include claims resulting from alcohol and drug abuse, self-inflicted injury or 
attempted suicide, war or civil commotion or the failure to follow appropriate 
medical advice.  Claim payments may also not be made while the claimant is 
resident outside an agreed geographical location. 
A list of common exclusions is given in the Chapter 31 (Glossary) under ‘Exclusions’. 
(5) 
It is difficult to find objective criteria to determine eligibility under the definition of 
incapacity.  The failure of activities of daily living (ADLs) may be deemed an 
objective test of incapacity, but the inability to continue in one’s own occupation 
and trade may be difficult to assess.   
The definition of disability must be a balance between the desire to meet the needs of 
genuine claimants and the desire to have in place a statistically sound and controllable 
insurance risk.  The advantages and disadvantages of the various ways of defining 
disability (incapacity) were discussed in Chapter 1. 


--- PAGE 227 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 21 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
In attempting to provide a regular income at a percentage of a claimant’s pre‐disability 
salary, careful wording is needed to avoid a scenario where policyholders, supported by 
their doctors and encouraged by worsening economic conditions, can begin claims for 
replacement income and continue it despite being much recovered.   
Insurers have tried to make the criteria more objective and more focused on work by 
developing and adopting other tests.  ADWs (activities of daily working), FATs (functional 
assessment tests) and PCAs (personal capability assessments) were mentioned in 
Chapters 1 and 2. 
5.3 
Critical illness insurance 
Simple … 
Critical illness insurance attempts to overcome some of this complexity by providing a fixed 
sum insured on certain pre-defined events.   
There are two main aspects to the simplicity of the product: 
(1) 
The lump sum payout – which cannot subsequently be withdrawn by the insurer – 
has a powerful draw for purchasers who are wary of insurers’ promises to look after 
them.   
(2) 
The claims trigger of diagnosis or procedure is easy to explain and adds to the 
attraction of the product. 
So CI is a theoretically simple product.  This is part of its attraction to consumers.   
… or not? 
But despite its success in overcoming certain complexities, and its comparative attraction 
to the public, it does not meet all the ill-health needs of policyholders. 
The product is still subject to exclusions and point-of-claim underwriting.  Definitions (of 
heart attack, for example) may differ between insurers in the same market – though in some 
territories work has been done to standardise definitions – whether for marketing or cultural 
reasons, and this can complicate claims acceptance.   
In addition, certain definitions may require evidence that the actual condition covered is 
more severe than the layperson’s normal understanding of the term used in the headline 
title of the condition. 
Tiered benefits 
A tiered benefit CI product is a step back towards more complex product structure. 
It is undoubtedly a more complex product and one that is difficult to compare with other 
(tiered or level) CI insurance products; this may reduce its appeal to prospective 
policyholders or to their financial advisers.   
In addition, there is potential for a higher degree of claims dispute.   


--- PAGE 228 ---
Page 22 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
5.4 
Long‐term care insurance 
The basic concept of long-term care insurance is fairly straightforward.   
However the specifics of a particular policy may cause considerable confusion. 
There may be a tendency for customers to believe that long‐term care insurance policies will 
cover the costs of care in old age. 
However, most policies offer cash rather than care, and the adequacy of the cash amount to 
meet healthcare needs many years ahead is not guaranteed.  Benefits may be payable for a 
limited period or may be subject to a maximum amount, when the need may last for a 
lifetime.  Different benefit levels and increased rates may be available to help approximate 
to these potential future expenses; but this can further complicate the product for a less 
expert purchaser. 
There is a big danger that the purchaser of long‐term care insurance may believe that care is 
being bought and may later be disappointed with what the plan provides. 
The trigger points for long-term care benefit are generally defined in terms of activities of 
daily living (ADLs) or cognitive impairment.  Examples of ADLs are around ‘bathing’, 
‘dressing’, ‘eating’ etc.  These need to be explained to the applicant, to avoid the belief that 
payment will follow a doctor’s referral. 
5.5 
Private medical insurance 
The private medical insurance concept is clear and simple – indemnification of medical 
costs incurred while insured.  However the conditions that may be imposed can complicate 
matters. 
For example, a condition such as breast cancer may be covered while it remains in an acute and 
treatable phase; but the treatment costs may not be covered if the disease becomes chronic and 
life threatening. 
Underwriting and acceptance 
The underwriting and acceptance process can be misunderstood.  The process by which an 
individual becomes accepted for insurance will vary by market and often also by insurers 
within the same market.  This is particularly relevant in respect of the applicant’s medical 
history. 
Medical underwriting is covered in Chapter 27. 
Policy conditions 
Limits / exclusions can further confuse.  Plans are seldom identical across a market.  The 
process of indemnification may be subject to certain upper limits or excesses where the 
patient has to meet part of the cost.  Certain treatments may be excluded from coverage. 


--- PAGE 229 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 23 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
Limits may be imposed on the total payments or the number of treatments for an episode of 
illness or on the total payments in each policy year.  These limits may apply to each benefit – for 
example up to £3,000 for physiotherapy in any policy year or up to 10 days of home nursing 
following a spell in hospital.  Alternatively they may apply in the aggregate, ie for all benefits in 
the period.   
The treatment of chronic conditions, pre‐existing conditions and treatment designed to improve 
the policyholder’s lifestyle rather than treat a medical condition (eg cosmetic surgery) would 
usually be excluded. 
Cost sharing 
Policies will sometimes have intricate member cost-sharing arrangements, which are not 
always well understood by the policyholder.  For example, policies may have one or a 
combination of individual service coinsurance and deductibles / excesses by policy year. 
Recall that coinsurance is where the policyholder pays a percentage of every claim cost, while an 
excess / deductible is the amount of loss that the policyholder has to incur before the insurer will 
pay anything on a claim. 
Pre‐authorisation 
The insurer may require the patient to receive authorisation (from the insurer) before 
treatment begins, both to ensure that the procedure is covered, and the medical facility is 
appropriate under the policy.  This may be deemed to restrict freedom and may not have 
been understood at the outset. 
However, this procedure should reduce the number of disputed claims, ensuring that 
policyholders understand the extent of their cover before treatment begins. 
Hospital bands / networks 
Some policies provide cover explicitly only in certain types of medical establishments 
(where costs are cheaper or where the insurer has a special arrangement with the provider 
and this has been reflected in the premium charged).  Again, this might not have been fully 
appreciated at purchase. 
In many markets, the policyholder’s doctor acts as an initial ‘gatekeeper’ in that treatments 
covered must be recommended by the doctor or by a consultant to whom the doctor has referred 
the policyholder. 
Pre‐authorisation by the insurer is an additional step.  This ensures that the policy covers the 
recommended treatment and the circumstances in which it is going to be provided.  Some policies 
will restrict cover to a hospital with which the insurer has negotiated special prices for treatment. 
This procedure also gives the insurer some control over the standard of treatment and care that 
the hospital will provide.  This may help to minimise the chance of complaints by the policyholder 
about the treatment received. 
 
 


--- PAGE 230 ---
Page 24 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
6 
Guarantees and reviewability of premiums and benefits 
6.1 
Introduction 
Part of the attraction of a traditional life insurance product is the fixed relationship between 
benefit and premium.  The variable nature and definition problems of healthcare insurance, 
and the high number of factors that can influence the experience, are more suited to a 
reviewable premium approach, ie the insurer has the right to review the premium to be 
charged for benefits in the period ahead.  The policyholder then has the option to accept or 
decline the proposed terms.   
Uncertainty as to the amount of future premiums is rarely what the policyholder will choose 
to meet needs.  However, when given the alternative, a higher starting premium to 
encompass the cost of guarantee, the applicant can make a more informed choice.  
Unfortunately, guarantees are not always available in some forms of healthcare insurances 
and not all insurers will offer both for immediate comparison. 
It is nonetheless very important that, where a reviewable policy is advised, the customer is 
clearly aware of this fact and has some understanding of the potential for premium change 
over the policy being considered. 
6.2 
Income protection insurance 
The cost of IP guarantees must be funded.  The insurer’s claim experience is subject to 
many influences totally outside the insurer’s control, eg economic and social influences, 
such as unemployment trends.  Even if experience turns out to be as expected, the 
guarantees are expensive because the insurer is required to set reserves to ensure that 
benefits will still be met if experience deteriorates.   
Guarantees provide consumers with the certainty of known future premium outgo – but that 
certainty comes at a price.   
With reviewability the insurer has the comfort of knowing that premiums may be adjusted in 
the light of emerging experience, although in practice there will be a limit as to how far 
increases may be imposed.  A doubling of premiums for example may dissuade the lives 
with better experience from renewing their policies, leaving the portfolio with a greater 
proportion of the potentially higher claimants, prompting an even bigger premium increase.  
Capping increases at new business rates should reduce the number of exits – if they still 
want cover, they can't go elsewhere more cheaply and hence are more likely to stay. 
6.3 
Critical illness insurance 
When CI was originally launched, many insurers kept the premiums reviewable because of 
the unknown nature of future risks.  Competition subsequently pushed some insurers into 
offering guarantees, on benefits or premiums, at little extra cost.  Possibly only now, the 
true cost of reserving for those future risks is being estimated and, where guarantees are 
still offered, a more appropriate differential is charged.   
With trends in incidence for each of the various critical illnesses covered moving in 
different directions, the increases on future reviews are difficult to predict.  It is possible 
that ongoing enhancements to reserves for guaranteed products may be significant. 


--- PAGE 231 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 25 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
Question 
If premiums are guaranteed, how is the insurer going to find such additional reserves? 
Solution 
In a proprietary company, the shareholders will ultimately provide the additional capital. 
In a mutual company the other policyholders who are members of the society (ie entitled to a 
share of its profits) will ultimately provide the additional funds needed. 
However, in either case, other (more immediate) sources of capital may be found, eg from 
reinsurance arrangements. 
 
The customer is probably not aware of this potential downside for the insurer.  Competition 
for new business and policyholder retention has not deterred insurers in many markets 
from making significant increases in premiums.  Insurers’ previous generosity in adding 
further diseases / benefits to existing policies at no additional cost is disappearing. 
The future is uncertain.  It may bring about a situation where the insurer is forced to 
continue to offer coverage even though illnesses, by the progress of medical science, are 
no longer critical and where the insurer is forced legally to cover less severe incidences of 
named conditions than was assumed in the calculation of current premiums.   
6.4 
Long‐term care insurance 
Immediate needs LTCI 
Immediate needs plans present no problems of premium guarantees.  An income stream is 
bought by a single premium.  The income stream may move to different levels with 
increasing disability and may be linked to some index.  It will be guaranteed to continue for 
the beneficiary’s lifetime and there may be some capital protection. 
Pre‐funded LTCI 
For other types of LTCI plans, reviewable products predominate.  The degree of premium 
variation at renewal will depend mainly on the past and future expected claims experience 
of the insurer and on estimated trends in the index (if any) to which the policy is linked.    
6.5 
Private medical insurance 
Private medical insurance is almost invariably a one-year product and as such is annually 
renewable.  There are some instances of products with premium guarantees for very limited 
periods (maybe three or five years), available at times when rates of medical inflation are 
deemed to be stable and easy to estimate.  The indemnity nature of this product, uncertainty 
over future trends and the lack of control over the costs charged by the supplier of the 
service in some markets, make guarantees very difficult to price effectively.   
As a one-year general insurance product, the insurer may refuse coverage in any future 
period; but in practice this rarely happens.  In the UK, individual PMI and major medical 
expense products generally (but not always) give guaranteed renewability within the policy 
conditions, but normally not guaranteed premiums.

Chapter 6 Summary 
Customers are among those who have a stake or interest in the design of health and care 
insurance products.  Their primary interests are to have products that meet their needs, are 
clear in purpose, have an appropriate charging structure (eg guarantees and options), and 
are affordable. 
Meeting specific needs 
IP insurance may be used to cover day‐to‐day expenses, loan servicing costs and other 
insurance premiums.  The need will be greatest where employer and State benefits are low.   
The main customers for IP are individuals (in particular the selcf‐employed), employers and 
small professional practices. 
CI insurance meets a variety of needs: 
 
the benefit can be used to repay a mortgage or other loan 
 
medical costs can be funded when the CI requires surgery or treatment 
 
income can be provided when the individuals cannot work as a result of their CIs 
 
business partners can purchase CI policies on the lives of each other such that the 
benefits will fund the buyout of the stake in the partnership 
 
it can fund a change of lifestyle in order to improve the claimant’s health 
 
other needs include recuperation or rehabilitation treatment after illness, taxation 
planning and medical aids. 
The needs met by tiered CI benefits are that they: 
 
may be a closer fit to medical distress and financial needs 
 
may be deemed more comprehensive and more fair. 
LTCI meets the need of financial protection against the costs of care and assistance when a 
person becomes unable to look after him/herself.  The care needs in old age may include 
domestic support, live‐in care, residential care and medical care. 
Demand for LTCI will depend on the level of State benefits and also the extent to which care 
may be provided informally (by family and friends). 
PMI aims to assist the policyholder in covering the costs of medical care.  Products should be 
designed to complement or augment the care offered by the State.  This means that 
different designs will be needed in different markets, eg if no State‐funded healthcare is 
provided, PMI may also cover primary care.   
PMI will be purchased by those who want better standards of treatment or care, more 
choice or shorter waiting times. 
Microinsurance targeted towards those with low incomes (eg in the developing world), and 
has a greater emphasis on the more basic components of cover and on affordability.   


--- PAGE 234 ---
Page 28 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
 
Chapter 6 Summary continued
Providing cash 
Cash gives the policyholder choice, both in terms of: 
 
the level of benefit 
 
the use of the benefit. 
However, this choice may not be positive if the policyholder makes a poor choice.  An 
advantage of indemnity benefits is that the insurer may be able to obtain cheaper prices 
from healthcare providers due to the volume of business given to providers. 
IP insurance provides a cash (income) benefit.  The policyholder chooses the amount of 
benefit, subject to limits.  A proportional or rehabilitation benefit may be provided so that 
the policyholder has an incentive to return to work. 
CI insurance provides a lump sum benefit that is not related to the actual loss incurred by the 
policyholder and it can be used for any purpose.  Benefits can be more than the cost of 
immediate care; the surplus may be used for recuperation / rehabilitation.  Sometimes the 
benefit is a genuine windfall to the policyholder (which can cause anti‐selection). 
LTCI is likely to provide cash benefits: while consumers may prefer indemnity benefits, 
insurers have found it increasingly difficult to assess the main risks (longevity (annuity) risk 
and the risk of benefit inflation being greater than expected). 
PMI policies are normally written on an indemnity basis, although there may be a cap on the 
benefits paid for certain procedures.  The insurer usually pays the provider of treatment 
directly.  Exceptions to this include cash benefits that are paid when a State‐alternative is 
used and health cash plans, which provide cash benefits. 
Providing peace of mind 
For many individuals there is a positive psychological effect of knowing they are protected 
should they suffer disability / sickness. 
IP insurance – The main concern is being unable to provide for self / dependants and/or to 
pay off loans if unable to work due to ill‐health.  State disability allowances are often 
insufficient and employment benefits are often only short term. 
CI insurance – The main concern is being diagnosed with certain headline diseases or the 
need to undergo serious surgical procedures. 
LTCI – The main concerns are having insufficient funds to pay for care in old age and that 
care available from the State (or informally) is inadequate.  The concern may also come from 
a family member or friend. 
PMI – The main concerns are that the costs of treatment are unaffordable or that the quality 
of State provision is inadequate. 


--- PAGE 235 ---
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
 
Page 29 
The Actuarial Education Company 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
 
 
 
Chapter 6 Summary continued
Simplicity and clarity 
IP insurance – The concept of IP is simple and appealing, but, there are certain (potentially 
confusing) issues: 
 
it may be necessary to apply benefit limits  
 
payouts are not always linked to current salary 
 
underwriting can be complex (due to occupation or past medical history) 
 
there may be exclusions, eg claims resulting from alcohol and drug abuse 
 
the definition of incapacity and the measures of fitness to work are not always open 
to objective assessment. 
CI insurance – These policies appeal to consumers as they are readily understood, provide a 
lump sum benefit that cannot subsequently be withdrawn and cover a risk that customers 
can recognise readily.  However confusion can arise due to exclusions, point‐of‐claim 
underwriting and non‐standard claim definitions. 
Tiered benefits CI: 
 
is more complex than a standard CI contract, making it hard to compare 
 
has potential for a higher degree of claims dispute. 
LTCI – While the concept is straightforward, confusion may arise over the extent of the 
benefits.  Cash benefits may not meet the costs of care, and may be subject to maximum 
amounts or payment period.  Also, trigger events are usually defined in terms of ADLs, which 
need to be explained to an applicant. 
PMI – Again, the concept is clear, but policy conditions can complicate the product and lead 
to misunderstandings by policyholders.  In particular: 
 
the underwriting and acceptance process can be misunderstood 
 
there may be limits on the benefits (eg excesses and upper limits) 
 
there may be exclusions (eg for pre‐existing and non‐acute conditions) 
 
there may be complicated cost‐sharing arrangements for sharing the risk between 
the policyholder and the insurer  
 
claims may be required to be pre‐authorised 
 
the choice of hospitals or other establishments in which treatment can be provided 
may be restricted. 


--- PAGE 236 ---
Page 30 
SP1‐06: Product design and stakeholder interests (1) 
© IFE: 2019 Examinations 
The Actuarial Education Company 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Chapter 6 Summary continued
Guarantees and reviewability of premiums and benefits 
Policyholders will prefer a guaranteed premium, however, high levels of uncertainty for 
health and care insurance means that guaranteed premium business can be expensive. 
IP insurance – Premiums may be guaranteed or periodically reviewable, however, claims 
experience is uncertain and subject to many influences outside the insurer’s control 
(eg unemployment trends) so guaranteed premiums may be expensive.  If premiums are 
reviewable then selective lapsing will act to limit the size of any premium increase. 
CI insurance – What are serious illnesses, and how they are treated, changes rapidly with 
medical advance.  This has made it difficult for insurers to assess the risks they face.  This 
uncertainty makes reviewable premiums more suitable, however, competitive pressures 
have led to guaranteed premium business being sold.   
LTCI – Immediate needs products present no problems for premium guarantees.  For 
pre‐funded products, reviewable premiums predominate.   
PMI – PMI is short term and usually annually renewable, and so premiums (and sometimes 
policy terms and conditions) can be changed each year.

The Actuarial Education Company
